Afghanistan 33
Da Afġānistān Islāmī Imārat · South-Central Asia · Capital: Kabul
Central Asia

At a Glance

Capital: Kabul — lies in a valley of the Hindu Kush. (4)

Government: Controlled by the Taliban; flag changed to Arabic letters on white. President Ashraf Ghani fled in 2021. (3)

Languages: Official languages Dari and Pashto. (1)

Borders: Pakistan to S/E (along the Durand Line); the Wakhan Corridor narrow strip connects Afghanistan to China. (8)

Physical Geography

Hindu Kush — dominant mountain range; western end inside Afghanistan; contains the Wakhan Corridor, Badakhshan region, and peaks Tirich Mir and Noshaq; the Khyber Pass crosses it. (5)

Wakhan Corridor — 11-mi-wide salient in Badakhshan Province created as a 'Great Game' buffer between Russian and British empires (1895); contains Lake Zorkul, Noshaq peak, and the Wakhjir Pass (largest time-zone jump in the world); bounded by the Pamir and Panj Rivers. (5)

Khyber Pass — strategic mountain pass on the Pakistan border; through the Safed Koh (Spin Gar) range; eastern end at Peshawar; western end at Jalalabad; lies above Jamrud Fortress; famed by Kipling as a 'sword cut'; site of the Torkham Highway. (4)

Hari River (Rudkaneh-ye Hari Rud) — Herat lies on it; flows NW into Turkmenistan, watering the breadbasket region. (1)

Cities & Regions

Kabul — capital; in a Hindu-Kush valley; Hamid Karzai International Airport site of an Islamic-State-Khorasan attack. (2)

Kandahar — cultural center of the Pashtun ethnic group; second-largest city. (2)

Herat — third-largest city, in the far NW; Tajik-majority; on the Hari River; once a Timurid capital; Musalla Complex razed in the 1885 'Panjdeh incident' (Great Game). (3)

Mazar-e-Sharif — in Balkh province; Shrine of Hazrat Ali with twin blue domes. (2)

Kunduz — northern city. (1)

Bagram Airfield — large U.S. military base in Parwan province (now Taliban-held). (1)

Bamiyan Valley — once held giant standing Buddha statues; destroyed by the Taliban in 2001; cultural center of the Hazara ethnic group. (2)

Kabul–Kandahar highway — major resurfacing project (Louis Berger Group); site of attacks. (1)

People

Pashtuns — majority ethnic group; folk dance is the attan; Sharbat Gula ('Afghan Girl' on the iconic National Geographic cover) is Pashtun. (1)

Hazaras — Shia Muslim minority; concentrated in Daikundi, Bamyan, and Ghor provinces; some Behsud tribe. (1)

Tajiks — dominate Balkh and Herat provinces. (2)

Alabama 21
'The Yellowhammer State' · Deep South · Capital: Montgomery
United States (States)

At a Glance

Capital: Montgomery — Dexter Avenue Baptist Church (MLK's pulpit); state capitol; Civil Rights Memorial ('until justice rolls down like waters'). (1)

Largest city: Birmingham — historic steel city; Rickwood Field (oldest baseball park in the US). (1)

Other cities: Huntsville (rocket/space industry; Marshall Space Flight Center), Mobile (Gulf port). (1)

Geography

Mobile Bay — Gulf of Mexico bay; receives the Alabama River; the Battle of Mobile Bay in 1864. (1)

Alabama River — namesake river; formed by the Coosa and Tallapoosa. (1)

Chattahoochee River — eastern border with Georgia. (1)

Civil Rights & Heritage

Selma to Montgomery marches (1965) — crossed the Edmund Pettus Bridge. (1)

Talladega Superspeedway — NASCAR oval; built on the former Anniston Air Force base; legendarily 'cursed' (Bobby Isaac's mid-race retirement); also home of the 2021 'Let's Go Brandon' chant origin. (2)

Snake-handling churches — rural Appalachian Pentecostal tradition documented in Salvation on Stone Mountain. (1)

Alaska 52
'The Last Frontier' · Pacific Far North · Capital: Juneau
United States (States)

At a Glance

Capital: Juneau — accessible only by air or sea. (1)

Largest city: Anchorage — Cook Inlet city; site of the 1964 Good Friday earthquake. (1)

Largest US state by area (more than twice the size of Texas). (1)

Purchased from Russia — 1867 'Seward's Folly' for $7.2 million. (1)

Sitka — former Russian-America capital (Novo-Arkhangelsk); oldest Orthodox cathedral in the Americas; largest US city by area; on Baranof Island and Chichagof Island. (1)

Geography

Denali (Mount McKinley) — 20,310 ft; highest peak in North America. (1)

Aleutian Islands — long volcanic chain off the southwest tip (formerly the Catherine Archipelago); contain the Near Islands, Rat Islands, Fox Islands; Attu, Kiska (WWII Japanese-occupied), Unalaska, Amaknak Island. (2)

Alexander Archipelago — southeastern panhandle islands; includes Sitka and Juneau areas. (1)

Kodiak Island — large southern island; Karluk River; Fort Abercrombie; brown-bear habitat. (1)

Yukon River — flows from Canada through Alaska to discharge near Kusilvak south of Norton Sound; Yup'ik name 'Kuigpak'; historic gold-rush route; Dawson City, Whitehorse upstream in Yukon Territory. (1)

Beaufort Sea / Chukchi Sea — Arctic Ocean coasts. (1)

Bering Strait — separates Alaska from Russia; the Diomede Islands (Big Diomede in Russia, Little Diomede in Alaska) sit in the middle. (1)

Parks & Wildlife

Denali National Park — Alaska's iconic park. (1)

Wrangell-St. Elias National Park — largest US national park. (1)

Glacier Bay National Park — calving tidewater glaciers; UNESCO site. (1)

Katmai National Park — on Kodiak; brown-bear viewing at Brooks Falls. (1)

Economy

Alaska Permanent Fund — every Alaskan resident receives an annual dividend from oil revenues. (1)

Prudhoe Bay — largest US oil field; source of the Trans-Alaska Pipeline. (1)

Albania 9
Republika e Shqipërisë · Balkans · Capital: Tirana
Balkans

At a Glance

Capital: Tirana — capital since 1920. (2)

Region: On the Balkan Peninsula (Ottoman name: 'Rumelia'). Separated from Italy by the Strait of Otranto. (3)

Coast: Southern coast on the Ionian Sea; western coast on the Adriatic Sea. (2)

Language: Albanian, divided into Gheg (north) and Tosk (south) dialects. (1)

Tirana

Skanderbeg Square — main civic plaza, named for the national hero. (2)

Et'hem Bey Mosque — landmark Ottoman-era mosque in central Tirana. (2)

Lana River runs through the city. (2)

Dajti Mountain — overlooks the city, known for hiking and cable-car views. (2)

Physical Geography

Lake Skadar — eastern end lies in Albania (rest in Montenegro). Lake Ohrid is shared with North Macedonia. Lake Prespa lies near the southern border. (2)

Albanian Alps (Prokletije / 'Accursed Mountains') — drained by the Ibar, Moraca and Drin Rivers; peaks include Đeravica, Zla Kolata, Maja Jezercë; site of gjakmarrja blood-feud customs governed by the Canon of Lekë Dukagjini. (1)

Other Cities & Economy

Shkodër — northern city with historic ruins. (1)

Fier — center of the oil and gas industry. (1)

Alberta 9
Prairie province · Capital: Edmonton · Largest city: Calgary
Canada (Provinces & Territories)

At a Glance

Capital: Edmonton — Alberta's second-largest city; home of the Al-Rashid Mosque, the first mosque ever built in Canada; light-rail line will connect to the largest mall in North America. (1)

Largest city: Calgary — at the confluence of the Bow and Elbow rivers; first Canadian city to host the Winter Olympics (1988); Saddledome arena, Brookfield Place, Bankers Hall; HQ of WestJet. (4)

Other cities: Lethbridge, Red Deer, Medicine Hat, Fort McMurray (oil-sands hub), Grande Prairie (Upper Peace Region). (2)

Politics: Alberta separatism produced the Maverick Party ('Wexit'); Kananaskis will host the 2025 G7 Summit. (1)

Mountains & Parks

Canadian Rockies — Alberta's western backbone, shared with BC. (1)

Banff National Park — Canada's oldest; Lake Louise (Fairmont Château), Moraine Lake, Castle Mountain (WWI Ukrainian internment camp), Lake Minnewanka, Mount Norquay ski resort; on the Bow River; Icefields Parkway connects to Jasper. (5)

Jasper National Park — Mount Edith Cavell (named for the WWI nurse), Maligne Lake with Spirit Island, Maligne Canyon karst formed by Medicine Lake outflow, Miette Hot Springs, the downward-flowing Angel Glacier, Mount Athabasca. (3)

Waterton Lakes National Park — adjacent to Glacier (Montana); part of the world's first International Peace Park. (1)

Wood Buffalo National Park — largest national park in Canada. (1)

Dinosaur Provincial Park — UNESCO badlands site near the Red Deer River. (1)

Economy

Athabasca Oil Sands — vast oil-sands deposits centered on Fort McMurray. (1)

Natural gas — concentrated around Medicine Hat. (1)

Algeria 25
El Djazâïr · Largest country in Africa by area · Capital: Algiers
Middle East & North Africa

At a Glance

Capital: Algiers — on the Mediterranean coast. (5)

Distinction: Largest country in Africa by area; the largest of the historical Barbary Coast. (1)

Position: Intersection of the Tropic of Cancer and the Prime Meridian lies in this country. (1)

Borders: Tunisia (E), Libya (E), Niger/Mali (S), Mauritania/Western Sahara/Morocco (W). (2)

Physical Geography

Atlas Mountains — span Algeria with Morocco and Tunisia; the Aurès Mountains are the eastern continuation; the Tell Atlas runs near the coast; the god Atlas of Greek myth gives the range its name. (4)

Ahaggar Mountains — in the south; contain Mt. Tahat, the country's highest point. (1)

Sahara Desert — covers most of Algeria; includes the two great sand-seas (ergs): Grand Erg Occidental and Grand Erg Oriental; the Tademait Plateau has star and barchan dunes. (2)

M'zab Valley — five fortified villages collectively called the Pentapolis; Ghardaïa is a notable M'zab city. (2)

Algiers

Set on a hill above the Mediterranean; iconic Casbah old town; Cathedral of Notre Dame d'Afrique (Neo-Byzantine, a relic of French colonization). (3)

Battle of Algiers (1956–57) — French counter-insurgency in the war for independence; subject of Gillo Pontecorvo's 1966 film The Battle of Algiers (Golden Lion). (3)

Algeria's pied-noir French-descended population fled after independence in 1962. (1)

Once ruled by a Dey; Miguel de Cervantes was held captive here. (1)

World's tallest minaret towers above Algiers (Djamaâ el Djazaïr mosque). (1)

Other Cities

Oran — major port on the Mediterranean; setting of Albert Camus' The Plague; site where Churchill destroyed the French fleet at Mers-el-Kébir in 1940; oil/gas industry centered here (state company Sonatrach in the suburb of Bir El-Djir). (3)

Constantine and Annaba — major eastern cities. (2)

Évian Accords (1962) — signed in France, recognized Algerian independence. (2)

Amazonas 7
State of Brazil · Largest state by area · Capital: Manaus
Brazil (States & Regions)

Manaus

Capital: Manaus — largest city in the Amazon Rainforest; nicknamed the 'Paris of the Tropics' because of its 19th-century rubber boom. (4)

Meeting of Waters — Manaus sits at the confluence of the dark Rio Negro and the muddy Amazon (Solimões). (2)

Journalist Phelippe Daou Bridge — only road crossing of the Rio Negro, linking Manaus to Iranduba. (2)

Amazonas Opera House — pink rubber-boom landmark on São Sebastião Square; featured in Fitzcarraldo. (1)

Anísio Jobim Penitentiary — site of a 2017 prison riot that killed 56. (1)

COVID-19 era — Washington Post reporting documented the difficult logistics of supplying the city with oxygen during the pandemic. (1)

State Geography

Pico da Neblina — Brazil's highest point, in the state's portion of the Guiana Highlands. (1)

Mount Curicuriari ('Mountain of the Sleeping Beauty') — in the Cabeça do Cachorro region. (1)

Anavilhanas National Park — world's largest river archipelago, on the Rio Negro. (1)

River ports: Itacoatiara, Parintins. (1)

Amazon Basin

Amazon River — flows through Amazonas before reaching Pará state; world's largest by discharge; mouth at Marajó Island. (2)

Rio Negro — major tributary; connects northward to the Orinoco River through the natural Casiquiare Canal. (2)

Roosevelt's River of Doubt — explored by Teddy Roosevelt and Cândido Rondon; he nearly died on the expedition. (1)

Andaman & Nicobar Islands 18
Union Territory in the Bay of Bengal · Capital: Port Blair
India (States & Regions)

At a Glance

Capital: Port Blair — on the Andaman Islands. (6)

Status: Union Territory of India, in the eastern Bay of Bengal / Andaman Sea. (5)

Two island groups: Andaman Islands (N), Nicobar Islands (S), separated by the Ten Degree Channel. (4)

Highest point: Saddle Peak (Andamans); also home of the creator god Puluga worshipped by local tribes. (1)

Andaman Islands

Chola dynasty referred to this area as Timaittivu; medieval travellers (Marco Polo, Ibn Battuta, Friar Odoric, John de Mandeville) described inhabitants as 'dog-headed cannibals'. (2)

Port Blair was a British penal colony (originally on Chatham Island, later Port Cornwallis); during WWII it briefly served as capital of Subhas Chandra Bose's Azad Hind government. (2)

Cleugh Passage separates Landfall Island from the rest; the Coco Channel separates the Andamans from Myanmar. (1)

Anthropology — Alfred Radcliffe-Brown's 1906 Andaman fieldwork founded structural functionalism. (1)

Indigenous Peoples

Sentinelese — uncontacted Scheduled Tribe on North Sentinel Island; killed American missionary John Allen Chau in 2018; Maurice Vidal Portman once abducted six to Port Blair; first peaceful contact led by Triloknath Pandit. (3)

Onge and Jarawa — other Andamanese tribes, possibly related to the Sentinelese. (2)

Andamanese languages — Boa Sr., the last person who understood the dialect Bo, died in 2010. (1)

Nicobar Islands

Danish missionaries called them Frederiksøerne; Chollan settlers called them the 'Land of the Naked'; settlement was prevented by malaria on Komorta and Nancowry. (1)

Great Nicobar is home to the Shompen people, who speak a language sometimes grouped with Austroasiatic. (1)

Andaman Sea

Names the sea east of the islands; flooded by the Boxing Day Tsunami at resorts including Khao Lak and Phuket. (3)

The Irrawaddy River of Myanmar discharges into this sea; Thailand's Phuket lies along it. (5)

Andorra 14
Principat d'Andorra · Pyrenees microstate · Capital: Andorra la Vella
Europe

At a Glance

Capital: Andorra la Vella — the highest capital city in Europe. (3)

Government: Principality headed by two co-princes: the President of France and the Bishop of Urgell (Catalonia). (3)

Borders: Spain and France only. (3)

Setting: Landlocked microstate in the Pyrenees Mountains between Spain and France. (4)

Language: Catalan — official; Andorra is one of the Catalan-speaking territories. (1)

Distinction: 2013 study found Andorra had the highest life expectancy on earth (~81 years). (1)

Geography

Coma Pedrosa — highest peak in Andorra. (3)

Valira River (Gran Valira) — Andorra's largest river; flows through the capital. (4)

Grandvalira — major ski resort, named for the river; near the town of Encamp. (3)

Valley of Madriu — UNESCO-listed cultural landscape. (1)

Parishes

Andorra is divided into seven parishes (parròquies). Escaldes-Engordany is the most recent (1978); others include Ordino, Canillo, St. Julià de Lòria. (3)

Andorra la Vella

Casa de la Vall — historic seat of Andorra's high court; once held the Armory of the Seven Keys safe (openable only when all seven parishes were present). (3)

Església de Sant Esteve and Santa Coloma Church (Romanesque) — landmark churches. (3)

Plaça del Poble — central square. (1)

History & Other

Tradition holds a charter to the Andorran people was granted by Charlemagne after they fought in Cerdanya. (1)

Joined the United Nations in 1993. (1)

1972: prized statue of St. Merixtell lost in a fire. (1)

Angola 27
República de Angola · Lusophone Southern Africa · Capital: Luanda
Sub-Saharan Africa

At a Glance

Capital & largest city: Luanda — most populous Lusophone city in the Eastern Hemisphere. (6)

Language: Portuguese — Angola is a major Lusophone state in Africa. (2)

Currency: the kwanza (named for the river). (1)

Flag: red and black with a gear wheel and machete centered. (2)

Distinctive exclave: Cabinda — oil-rich enclave separated from the rest of Angola, located between the two Congos. (10)

Physical Geography

Bié Plateau — central highland; source of major rivers; city of Huambo (once Nova Lisboa under Portuguese rule) lies on it. (2)

Kwanza River — namesake of the currency; flows past Luanda's hinterland; receives the Cutato from the Bié Plateau. (3)

Cunene River — flows along the southern border with Namibia; pump station once watered the now-abandoned Tigres Island. (3)

Cubango / Okavango — the Cubango in Angola becomes the Okavango in Botswana, ending in the Okavango Delta. (2)

Moçâmedes (Namib) Desert — coastal desert continuing south into Namibia; contains Iona National Park. (2)

Tigres Island — became an island in the 1960s when the Atlantic broke through an isthmus. (2)

Benguela Current — cold ocean current joins Angola's coast around 16°S; drives the dryness of the Namib and the wreck-strewn Skeleton Coast to the south. (2)

Cabinda

Cabinda Province — Angola's oil-rich exclave, between the Republic of Congo and the DR Congo; once known as Portuguese Congo; formed from the merger of the N'Goyo, Loango, and Kakongo kingdoms via the Treaty of Simulambuco; capital also called Cabinda, on the Bele River. (4)

Treaty of Simulambuco and Treaty of Chinfuma transformed the N'Goyo kingdom into the territory. (1)

Luanda

Sits on the Ilha do Cabo Peninsula opposite the 20-mile-long Mussulo Peninsula. (2)

Fortaleza do São Miguel — star-shaped Portuguese fort; houses the Armed Forces Museum. (2)

Agostinho Neto International Airport (Quatro de Fevereiro) — base of national airline TAAG. (2)

Sonangol Group — state oil company; headquartered here. (2)

Chinese-built suburb of Quilamba Quiaxi (or 'Quilamba'). (2)

Economy & Society

ENDIAMA — state diamond company; operates the Catoca mine in Lunda Sul province. (1)

Benguela Railway — colonial-era line from the port of Lobito to the copper mines of central Africa. (1)

Kuduro — Angolan dance/music style blending tribal sounds with techno. (1)

Bantu languages dominate ethnically; provinces include Cunene (Ovambo-dominated) and Lunda Sul. (2)

Notable

Cameia and Mupa National Parks are major protected areas. (1)

Marburg virus outbreak in Uíge province killed 227 people (largest known outbreak of the virus). (1)

Antigua and Barbuda 3
Caribbean (Leeward Islands) · Capital: St. John's
Caribbean

At a Glance

Capital: St. John's — on Antigua; home to V.C. Bird International Airport and the Sandy Island lighthouse. (1)

Flag: centered on a stylized rising sun. (1)

Position: Caribbean island state in the Leeward Islands of the Lesser Antilles; close to neighbor Montserrat. (1)

Geography

Sherkeley Mountains — Antigua's mountain spine; extend from Johnsons Point to Falmouth Harbor; folklore tells of a soul-stealing figure above Cades Bay. (1)

Boggy Peak — Antigua's highest mountain; briefly renamed for Barack Obama. (1)

Pillars of Hercules — non-Mediterranean rock formations guarding an entrance on Antigua. (1)

Nelson's Dockyard — UNESCO World Heritage Georgian naval dockyard. (1)

Argentina 78
República Argentina · Southern South America · Capital: Buenos Aires
South America

At a Glance

Capital: Buenos Aires — on the western shore of the Río de la Plata, across from Montevideo. (12)

Flag: light-blue / white / light-blue triband with the Sun of May at its center; designed by Manuel Belgrano and made official by the Congress of Tucumán. (3)

Name origin: from Latin argentum ('silver') — the same root names this metal that was mined at Cerro Rico in Potosí. (1)

Extremes: contains both the highest point in the Western/Southern hemispheres (Mount Aconcagua) and the lowest (Laguna del Carbón). (2)

Borders: Chile (along the Andes), Bolivia, Paraguay, Brazil, and Uruguay; second-largest country in South America after Brazil. (6)

Physical Geography

Andes Mountains — form the western border with Chile; run the length of the country and host Mount Aconcagua, Llullaillaco, and the Cordillera Occidental; the world's longest mountain range, running Colombia → Argentina. (4)

Mount Aconcagua — highest mountain in the Southern and Western Hemispheres; near the Chile border; ascended via the Polish Glacier; west face features a 6,000-ft cliff above the Horcones Valley; the Canaleta couloir links the Cresta del Guanaco to its twin summits; name comes from Quechua 'stone sentinel'; cities Uspallata and Mendoza lie on its east flank; closest higher peak is Tirich Mir, over 10,000 mi away. (5)

Patagonia — vast, windy southern region shared with Chile; named by Antonio Pigafetta on Magellan's expedition (claimed to have seen 'giants'); home to the Mapuche and Selk'nam peoples; site of the Conquest of the Desert; contains the Perito Moreno Glacier (whose ruptures dam the Brazo Rico), Mt. Fitz Roy, the Valdés Peninsula (continent's lowest point), and Los Glaciares National Park; bounded north by the Colorado/Barrancas Rivers; subject of Bruce Chatwin's first book In Patagonia; setting of Saint-Exupéry's Night Flight; gave its name to the clothing company. (8)

Pampas — central grassland plains between the Río Colorado and the Salado Norte; home of the gauchos; eastern wet portion (cattle, sheep, estancias) runs Tandil → Mar del Plata; western dry portion (alfalfa, wheat) runs Bahía Blanca → Santa Fé; province of La Pampa has capital Santa Rosa; setting of the epic Martín Fierro. (3)

Gran Chaco — semi-arid lowlands in the north, shared with Bolivia and Paraguay; south of the Pantanal; Pilcomayo River runs through it; backdrop of the Chaco War (Royal Dutch Shell vs. Standard Oil proxy). (3)

Tierra del Fuego — archipelago at South America's southern tip, shared with Chile; largest island separated from Picton/Lennox/Nueva by the Beagle Channel; bounded south by the Drake Passage, north by the Strait of Magellan; Argentine province capital Ushuaia; contains Mount Krund (Cerro Castor ski resort), Fagnano Lake, and the Train of the End of the World; named for Tehuelche bonfires. (5)

Quebrada de Humahuaca — UNESCO gorge of the Río Grande north of San Salvador de Jujuy; ancient Incan trade route; village of Purmamarca known for badlands; extends from the Puña to La Quiaca (across from Villazón, Bolivia). (1)

Iguazú Falls — massive cataract system on a tributary of the Paraná on the Argentina–Brazil border; includes the Garganta del Diablo canyon; drew Eleanor Roosevelt's quip 'Poor Niagara'. (2)

Sand dunes — the world's tallest (named for Federico Kirbus) lies in Argentina's Catamarca Province. (2)

Rivers & Waterways

Río de la Plata — broad estuary forming the border with Uruguay; receives the Paraná and Uruguay Rivers; the Samborombón Bay is its inland-most portion; the Canal Punto Indio is its most navigable; Buenos Aires sits on its western shore. (6)

Paraná River — forms much of the eastern border with Paraguay; flows through the Paraná Delta; sourced in Mato Grosso, Brazil. (3)

Uruguay River — eastern border tributary of the Río de la Plata. (2)

Chubut River — Patagonian river dammed by the Florentino Ameghino Dam. (1)

Lake O'Higgins / San Martín — deepest lake in South America; shared with Chile; named after one of two major generals depending on which side you're on. (1)

Cities & Provinces

Buenos Aires — capital; residents are porteños; lies on the Río de la Plata across from Montevideo. Landmarks: Casa Rosada (Pink House, presidential mansion) on Plaza de Mayo; Teatro Colón opera house; the Kavanagh Building (tallest in Latin America in 1936) in the Retiro barrio; the extremely wide 9 de Julio Avenue with its 235-ft obelisk at Corrientes Avenue; Santiago Calatrava's Puente de la Mujer in Puerto Madero; the colorful Italian La Boca district at the Riachuelo (home of Boca Juniors); the Jewish quarter of Once; the Torre de la Inglaterra; the two-and-a-half-meter-wide Minimal House; Boca Juniors vs. River Plate rivalry; Mothers of the Plaza de Mayo protest group; Lunfardo Italian-based creole; Pope Francis was born here. (9)

Córdoba — second-largest city; named on the flag-clue question alongside Buenos Aires. (1)

Mendoza — wine-region city on the east flank of Aconcagua. (1)

Ushuaia — southernmost city; resort town on the Beagle Channel in Tierra del Fuego province. (2)

Formosa Province — northern province sharing a name with Taiwan. (1)

San Juan Province — contains Ischigualasto Provincial Park (UNESCO site with a complete Triassic fossil record and eoraptor/cynodont fossils), administered with adjacent Talampaya National Park. (1)

Baritu National Park — northern park home to particularly large cedrela trees. (1)

Los Glaciares National Park — contains the country's westernmost point. (1)

Ezeiza Airport — serves Buenos Aires. (1)

Disputed & Outlying Territories

Falkland Islands / Islas Malvinas — South Atlantic archipelago disputed with the UK; capital Stanley; site of the 1982 war after a referendum was rejected by Cristina Fernández de Kirchner's predecessors; East Falkland contains the Lafonia peninsula and Bodie Creek Suspension Bridge; Goose Green and Darwin Harbor named for the Beagle; tallest peak Mount Usborne; third-largest island Weddell Island (world's largest private island); RAF Mount Pleasant connects to Punta Arenas; Christ Church Cathedral in Stanley has a whalebone arch. (6)

Antarctic claims — Argentina claims part of the Antarctic Peninsula (overlapping Chilean and British claims) and the Weddell Sea beyond. (3)

People & Culture

Gauchos — South American cowboys of the Pampas; immortalized in the epic Martín Fierro; estancia ranching culture. (2)

Mapuche — indigenous people of Patagonia; subject of the Araucanización migration south. (2)

Tango — born in Buenos Aires; the march 'La Cumparsita' was popularized as tango in the early 20th century. (1)

Football — Argentina won the 2022 World Cup Final at Lusail; Diego Maradona's 'Hand of God' goal beat England at the Estadio Azteca in the 1986 quarterfinal; the 1986 and 1990 finals; the Boca Juniors / River Plate rivalry in Buenos Aires. (3)

Pope Francis — born Jorge Mario Bergoglio in Buenos Aires. (1)

Pampero winds — cause summer storms in Buenos Aires. (1)

Cueva de las Manos ('Cave of the Hands') — UNESCO site on the Río Pinturas in Patagonia; thousands of stenciled handprints and guanacos painted in kaolin, hematite and natrojarosite by the Toldense people; re-discovered in 1941 by Alberto Maria de Agostini. (2)

Arizona 47
'The Grand Canyon State' · Southwest US · Capital: Phoenix
United States (States)

At a Glance

Capital: Phoenix — in Maricopa County (largest US county to vote for Trump in 2016, once governed by Sheriff Joe Arpaio). (1)

Other major cities: Tucson, Mesa, Chandler, Scottsdale, Glendale (with the Westgate Entertainment District including State Farm Stadium and Desert Diamond Stadium, host of Super Bowl 57). (1)

Geography

Grand Canyon National Park — Arizona's most iconic feature; carved by the Colorado River. (1)

Sonoran Desert — covers the southern half; Saguaro National Park; the Yaqui and Tohono O'odham people are native; Organ Pipe Cactus National Monument at the Mexican border. (1)

Colorado Plateau — north and northeast; Painted Desert, Petrified Forest National Park. (1)

Lake Powell / Lake Mead — reservoirs on the Colorado River. (1)

Antelope Canyon — slot canyon on Navajo land near Page. (1)

Meteor Crater — well-preserved impact crater near Winslow. (1)

People

Navajo Nation — largest US tribal land; covers parts of Arizona, New Mexico, and Utah; mining in Shiprock; the 'Long Walk' deportation by Kit Carson. (1)

Hopi — pueblo people whose reservation is fully surrounded by the Navajo Nation. (1)

Border

Mexico-Arizona Border — runs past Organ Pipe Cactus National Monument; Nogales is the largest border city. (1)

Arkansas 25
'The Natural State' · South-Central US · Capital: Little Rock
United States (States)

At a Glance

Capital: Little Rock. (1)

Other cities: Fort Smith, Fayetteville, Bentonville (Walmart HQ), Hot Springs. (1)

Geography

Ozark Mountains — northwestern uplands; shared with Missouri. (1)

Ouachita Mountains — west-central range; contain Hot Springs National Park (Bertram Boltwood studied the radon-rich water; Bathhouse Row). (1)

Arkansas River — main river; flows southeast through Little Rock to the Mississippi. (1)

Crowley's Ridge — unusual narrow geological ridge that runs north-south. (1)

Marion — site of the Sultana shipwreck museum (the 1865 steamboat disaster killed over 1,000 returning Union soldiers). (1)

Heritage

Bill Clinton Presidential Library — in Little Rock. (1)

Crater of Diamonds State Park — only diamond-bearing site in the world open to public prospecting. (1)

Armenia 23
Republic of Armenia · South Caucasus / Transcaucasia · Capital: Yerevan
Caucasus

At a Glance

Capital: Yerevan — on the Hrazdan River, northeast of the Ararat Plain; one of the world's oldest continuously inhabited cities. (4)

Government: former Soviet Republic; in 2018 a Velvet Revolution ousted PM Serzh Sargsyan, replacing him with Nikol Pashinyan. (1)

Religion & Alphabet: Christianity first spread from the Khor Virap Monastery near Lusarat; the Armenian alphabet was developed by Mesrop Mashtots. (1)

Borders: Turkey, Iran (across the Aras River), Georgia, and rival neighbor Azerbaijan; smallest country of the Transcaucasia region. (4)

National Symbols & Disputes

Mount Ararat — Armenia's most powerful national symbol despite lying just across the border in Turkey (Ağrı / Iğdır Provinces); believed landing site of Noah's Ark; first ascended by Friedrich Parrot and Khachatur Abovian; appears on the Armenian Coat of Arms; divided into greater and lesser cones. (4)

Nagorno-Karabakh — autonomous region long disputed with Azerbaijan; the unrecognized Republic of Artsakh held it; the region shrank after Armenia's defeat in the 2020 war; mediation attempted via the Prague Process and Madrid Principles; the Lachin Corridor was given special status under the Bishkek Protocol; Stepanakert is its main city. (4)

Nakhchivan — Azerbaijani exclave sandwiched between Armenia and Iran; one of the two countries occupies the other's enclave village of Karki. (2)

Armenian Genocide — 20th-century Turkish atrocity; refugees gave Yerevan districts their names (Nork Marash, Malatya, Arabkir); the Armenian Quarter of Jerusalem is in part populated by descendants of survivors. (2)

Physical Geography

Caucasus Mountains — Armenia lies in the South Caucasus; the Armenian Highlands lie south of the main range; highest point Mt. Elbrus; legendary site of the Gates of Alexander. (2)

Geghama Mountains — volcanic range east of Yerevan offering hiking trails. (2)

Zangezur Mountains — form the border between Nakhchivan and Armenia's Syunik province. (1)

Lake Sevan — central lake in Gegharkunik Province; one-sixth of the country's area; the only remaining of historic Armenian 'seas' inside the borders; drained by the Hrazdan River and stabilized by a tunnel from the Arpa River; contains the Astvatsatsin Monastery founded by St. Mesrop Mashtots; largest lake in the Caucasus. (3)

Hrazdan River — drains Lake Sevan and flows through Yerevan; site of hydroelectric plants. (4)

Aras River — forms the borders with Iran and Turkey. (2)

Cities, Monasteries & Landmarks

Yerevan — capital; landmarks include Republic Square, the Cascade Complex, and archaeological sites like the Shengavit obelisk and a cuneiform inscription from King Argishti I of Urartu who founded a fortress here; the Dashnak party established a short-lived Democratic Republic here. (4)

Lori Province — home to the medieval Sanahin Monastery. (1)

Kotayk Province — contains the cliffside Geghard Monastery. (1)

Metsamor Nuclear Power Plant — produces roughly 40% of Armenia's electricity. (1)

Khor Virap Monastery — near Lusarat; traditional site where Christianity first took hold. (1)

Armenian Diaspora & Influence

Armenian Quarter of Jerusalem — Old City quarter accessed from the Jaffa Gate; Holy Translators' School was the city's first coeducational school; cathedral where the head of the apostle James is said to be buried (where Kanye West baptized his daughter in 2015); developed in part by Crusader queens Arda, Morphia, and Melisende. (1)

Glendale, California — between Burbank and Pasadena; houses St. Gregory the Illuminator Cathedral, the North American seat of the Armenian Catholic Church. (1)

Aghtamar Island in Lake Van (Turkey) — home to the Armenian Cathedral of the Holy Cross, built by Gagik I Artsruni. (1)

Julfa (in Nakhchivan) — once held the world's largest collection of khachkar gravestones before their destruction in the 1990s. (1)

Aruba 8
Country of the Kingdom of the Netherlands · Leeward Antilles · Capital: Oranjestad
Caribbean

At a Glance

Capital: Oranjestad — founded by the Dutch, like sister-island capital Willemstad on Curaçao. (1)

Status: constituent country of the Kingdom of the Netherlands; access via the international airport named for Queen Beatrix. (2)

Currency: the florin, though the U.S. dollar is accepted everywhere thanks to heavy American tourism. (1)

Languages: two officials — Dutch and Papiamento, a Spanish/Portuguese-derived creole. (2)

Position & Neighbors

ABC Islands — Aruba forms the A along with Bonaire and Curaçao in the Leeward Antilles of the Caribbean Sea. (4)

Caribbean Sea — Aruba sits in the sea bounded by the Greater and Lesser Antilles; nearby waters include the Gulf of Paria (connected by the Dragon's Mouths) and Shoal Bay. (1)

Dutch Caribbean — the wider Dutch colonial sphere also includes Saba, St. Eustatius, and the Dutch half of St. Martin; rhythmic tumba music originated in these colonies. (1)

Assam 10
Northeast India · Capital: Dispur (Guwahati)
India (States & Regions)

At a Glance

Capital: Dispur (a part of Guwahati, the state's principal city). (1)

Region: One of India's Seven Sister States of the northeast. (1)

Famous for: Assam tea and Assam silk. (1)

Adjacent: Bangladesh sits between Assam and Meghalaya, which contains Cherrapunji — once the wettest town on Earth. (2)

Brahmaputra River

Major Indian river flowing through Assam. Rises from the Angsi Glacier; upper Tibetan reach is the Yarlung Tsangpo. Enters India through Arunachal Pradesh and the Grand Canyon of the Tsangpo. (3)

Flows through Dibrugarh and Guwahati in Assam, then joins the Padma in Bangladesh; largest tributary of the Ganges; together they form the world's largest river delta in the Bay of Bengal. (2)

Kaziranga National Park

Protected reserve on the Brahmaputra known for its population of one-horned rhinoceroses. (1)

Tea & Other Notable Places

Darjeeling (in neighbouring West Bengal) produces a tea distinct from Assam's; both regions are famed worldwide. Darjeeling teas use Chinese Camellia sinensis plants. (2)

Kanchenjunga view — Assam's neighbor Sikkim hosts the mountain itself; nearby Darjeeling offers Satyajit-Ray-immortalised vistas. (1)

History

Burma Road / Ledo Road — Joseph Stilwell's WWII supply road extension began in Assam and crossed the Chindwin River; Japanese efforts to halt it culminated in the unsuccessful Battles of Kohima and Imphal. (1)

Atlantic Provinces 17
Nova Scotia · New Brunswick · Prince Edward Island · Newfoundland & Labrador
Canada (Provinces & Territories)

Nova Scotia

Capital: Halifax — Victorian harborfront capital. (1)

Cape Breton Island — northeastern island; site of the Alexander Graham Bell Historic Park. (2)

Bays & peninsulas: Aspotogan and Chebucto peninsulas bound St. Margaret's Bay; the Bay of Fundy has the world's highest tides; Cobequid Bay meets Truro. (1)

Sable Island — foggy crescent island (world's foggiest place) between the cold Labrador Current and warm Gulf Stream; once a walrus colony; famous wild horses. (2)

Annapolis Valley — site of the annual Apple Blossom Festival. (1)

Strait of Canso — separates Cape Breton from the mainland. (1)

New Brunswick

Capital: Fredericton — has the first English-language university in Canada (UNB). (1)

Moncton — largest city; in the Petitcodiac River valley. (1)

Bay of Fundy — borders the province; record tides. (1)

Confederation Bridge — links Cape Tormentine, NB to PEI across the Northumberland Strait. (1)

Prince Edward Island

Capital: Charlottetown. (1)

Geography: smallest Canadian province; sits in the Gulf of St. Lawrence; separated from the mainland by the Northumberland Strait. (1)

Summerside — second city, on the Northumberland Strait. (1)

Confederation Bridge — northern end on PEI. (1)

Newfoundland & Labrador

Capital: St. John's — easternmost city in North America. (1)

Newfoundland island: the Long Range Mountains stretch into the Northern Peninsula; Cape Spear is the easternmost point of North America (lighthouse on the Avalon Peninsula); St. Pierre and Miquelon (French overseas territory) lies offshore; native Beothuk people were exterminated by settlers. (4)

Gander International Airport — hosted diverted flights on 9/11. (1)

Gros Morne National Park — Canada's second-largest park; UNESCO site. (1)

Labrador — mainland region; Torngat Mountains in the north (sole armed Nazi presence in the Americas — weather station 'Kurt'); Smallwood Reservoir (world's 2nd-largest manmade lake) on the Churchill River; Happy Valley-Goose Bay is the main town. (3)

Strait of Belle Isle — separates Newfoundland from Labrador. (1)

Australia 150
Commonwealth of Australia · Oceania · Capital: Canberra
Oceania

At a Glance

Capital: Canberra — purpose-built in the Australian Capital Territory; the country is the largest with no land borders to any other. (2)

Six states + two territories: New South Wales, Victoria, Queensland, South Australia, Western Australia, Tasmania, plus the Northern Territory and the Australian Capital Territory. (4)

Symbols & sport: the boxing kangaroo Olympic mascot; the Barassi Line divides regions playing rugby league from Australian-rules football; the Melbourne Cricket Ground in Yarra Park is the Southern Hemisphere's largest stadium. (2)

Indigenous heritage: Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander peoples; the country was once part of the Sahul landmass connected to New Guinea. (3)

Physical Geography

Great Dividing Range — runs the length of the east coast through NSW, Victoria, and Queensland; only the Gulf of Carpentaria is the watershed west of it that reaches the ocean. (3)

Snowy Mountains — part of the Great Dividing Range in NSW; contain the highest peak Mt. Kosciuszko and Perisher Ski Resort (largest in the Southern Hemisphere). (3)

Blue Mountains — west of Sydney; demarcated by the Nepean and Hawkesbury Rivers. (3)

The Outback — the brutal arid interior; the Dingo Fence stretches across it; hosted British nuclear tests at Emu Field; bounded by 'the bush'; the Royal Flying Doctor Service covers it via the Stuart Highway; encompasses the Simpson, Great Victoria, Great Sandy, and Tanami Deserts and the Nullarbor Plain (longest stretch of straight railroad in the world). (4)

Uluru / Ayers Rock — giant arkose Bornhardt monolith in the Northern Territory; sacred to the Anangu people; shares a national park with Kata Tjuta ('the Olgas'); reached from Alice Springs; climbing now banned; the Mutitjulu watering hole has snake creation paintings. (3)

Murray–Darling system — country's longest river system; tributaries include the Mitta Mitta, Lachlan, Murrumbidgee, Loddon, Campaspe and Darling; the Murray flows into the Southern Ocean at Encounter Bay just south of Kangaroo Island, near Coorong National Park. (3)

Lake Eyre / Kati Thanda — country's largest lake; endorheic and seasonal; lowest continental point at -52 ft; fed by the Warburton and Diamantina Rivers; part of the Great Artesian Basin; Goyder Channel links its north and south sections; site of Donald Campbell's 1964 land speed record (Bluebird-Proteus CN7). (3)

Great Barrier Reef — world's largest reef system off the Queensland coast in the Coral Sea; over 1,000 mi long; 600 continental islands and 300 coral cays; threatened by climate change, crown-of-thorns starfish, bleaching, and runoff from the Fitzroy and Brisbane Rivers. (3)

Bass Strait — separates Tasmania from the mainland. (3)

Gulf of Carpentaria — between the Northern Territory and Queensland; inlet of the Arafura Sea; stretches Cape York Peninsula → Cape Arnhem; experiences the 'Morning Glory' roll cloud; largest island Groote Eylandt. (3)

Tasman Sea — between Australia's east coast and New Zealand. (2)

Wildlife & Environment

Mammals: world's largest country with extant monotremes (platypus, echidna); home to koalas, kangaroos, and the quokka (on Rottnest Island near Perth and Albany's Bald Island). (1)

Marsupial predators: Tasmanian devil (largest extant); the extinct thylacine ('Tasmanian tiger') once lived only in Australia and New Guinea. (1)

Antarctic & Overseas Territories

Australian Antarctic Territory — the world's largest Antarctic claim, based partly on Victorian frontage; Terre Adélie (French) separates its two sections; research stations include Mawson (named for explorer Douglas Mawson, whose namesake expedition included Lt. Ninnis of Ninnis Glacier). (1)

Heard and McDonald Islands — sub-Antarctic Australian island territory. (1)

Christmas Island — Indian Ocean territory; only outside link via Perth. (1)

Exploration & History

Burke and Wills expedition — attempted to cross the continent south-to-north; turned back by bad weather at the Flinders River. (1)

HMS Endeavour — Cook landed at Botany Bay in 1770; the Beagle also surveyed Australian shores. (1)

Van Diemen's Land — original name for Tasmania, renamed for a different Dutch explorer (Abel Tasman). (2)

Regional Files

New South Wales — capital Sydney; see India_… style breakout file `australia_new_south_wales.json`. (4)

Queensland — capital Brisbane; Great Barrier Reef, Gold Coast — `australia_queensland.json`. (5)

Victoria — capital Melbourne — `australia_victoria.json`. (2)

Western Australia — capital Perth — `australia_western_australia.json`. (2)

Northern Territory — capital Darwin; Uluru, Arnhem Land — `australia_northern_territory.json`. (5)

Tasmania — capital Hobart — `australia_tasmania.json`. (7)

South Australia — capital Adelaide; Gulf of St. Vincent, Mount Lofty Ranges, Barossa wine region, Kaurna people. (3)

Austria 37
Republik Österreich · Central Europe (Alpine) · Capital: Vienna
Europe

At a Glance

Capital: Vienna — landlocked German-speaking Alpine country; home to the second-largest German-speaking population of any city in the world. (6)

Government: nine-state federation; formerly united with Hungary in the Austro-Hungarian dual monarchy under Franz Joseph I (his name lives on as Russia's Franz Josef Land in the Arctic). (3)

States (Bundesländer): Burgenland, Carinthia, Lower Austria, Upper Austria (capital Linz), Salzburg, Styria (capital Graz), Tyrol (capital Innsbruck), Vorarlberg, Vienna. (3)

Physical Geography

Alps — dominate the country (along with Switzerland and Liechtenstein); peaks include the Jungfrau and Mont Blanc further west; subdivisions include the Ötztal (where 'Ötzi the Iceman' was found), Samtal, and Zillertal. (3)

Grossglockner — highest mountain in Austria since 1919; second-most-prominent peak in the Alps; in the Hohe Tauern range on the Carinthia–Tyrol border near Salzburg; Pasterze Glacier at its base is rapidly retreating; reached via the Grossglockner-Hochalpenstrasse; first summited in 1800 by the Klotz brothers. (2)

Wildspitze — second-highest peak; in Tyrol. (1)

Inn River — flows through Tyrol's capital Innsbruck (which gets its name from a bridge on the Inn); the only river in Switzerland that flows to the Black Sea; tributaries include the Salzach and Alz; passes through Braunau (Hitler's birthplace). (1)

Danube River — flows through Vienna. (1)

Rhine River — forms part of the borders with Liechtenstein and Switzerland. (1)

Lake Constance (Bodensee) — at the meeting of Austria, Germany, and Switzerland; shores include Bregenz and Lindau; the Rhine flows through it (Obersee/Untersee). (3)

Lake Neusiedl — famous wine lake of the Burgenland region. (1)

Vienna

Schönbrunn Palace — Habsburg Rococo summer palace in the Hietzing district; gardens include the Neptune Fountain (Wilhelm Bayer) and a maze by Jean Trehet; Tiergarten Zoo is the world's oldest continuously operating zoo; remodeled under Maria Theresa; houses the Great Gallery; Schönbrunn-Yellow is the namesake palette. (4)

Hofburg — central imperial palace; winter complement to Schönbrunn. (2)

Belvedere Palace — complex with the Palace Stables; houses Klimt's The Kiss. (2)

St. Stephen's Cathedral — Gothic landmark on Stephansplatz. (2)

Ringstrasse — grand boulevard built where mayor Karl Luger demolished the medieval walls; encloses the Innere Stadt. (2)

Spanish Riding School — exhibits the Lipizzaner stallions. (1)

Secession Building — 1890s exhibition hall. (1)

Hundertwasserhaus — apartment building with trees integrated into the design. (1)

Sigmund Freud Museum, Ankeruhr Clock on Hoher Markt, and the formerly Jewish Leopoldstadt district. (2)

Café Mozart and the Rathaus library (which holds the oldest recipe for apple strudel). (1)

OPEC is headquartered here even though Austria is not a member. (2)

Cities & Regions

Salzburg — fourth-largest city; birthplace of Mozart at Getreidegasse No. 9; Mirabell Gardens featured in The Sound of Music (former Trapp home); Residenzgalerie in the former Prince-Archbishops' palace. (2)

Innsbruck — capital of Tyrol; on the Inn River; near the Brenner Pass (where Hitler and Mussolini celebrated the Pact of Steel). (2)

Tyrol — western region; its southern portion (Alto Adige / Südtirol) was given to Italy after WWI. (1)

Linz — Upper Austria's capital; on the Danube. (1)

Graz — capital of Styria; second-largest city. (1)

Burgenland — sparsely populated easternmost state; wine country around Lake Neusiedl. (2)

Wachau region — apricot brandy Marillenbrand country along the Danube. (1)

Hallstatt — town in the Salzkammergut; home to the world's oldest working salt mine. (1)

Cuisine & Culture

Wiener Schnitzel — veal cutlet named for Vienna. (1)

Tortes — Central-European cakes; the Sacher Torte uses apricot jam (created for Prince Metternich), the Linzer Torte has a lattice top, and the Esterházy Torte is named for a Hungarian prince. (1)

Azerbaijan 30
Republic of Azerbaijan · South Caucasus / Transcaucasia · Capital: Baku
Caucasus

At a Glance

Capital: Baku — on the Absheron Peninsula of the Caspian Sea; lowest national capital in the world; capital of the first democratic republic in the Muslim world. (6)

Government: former Soviet republic ruled by Ilham Aliyev (son of Heydar Aliyev, namesake of the Zaha Hadid-designed Heydar Aliyev Center). (2)

Religion: northernmost Shi'a-majority country in the world. (2)

Borders: Iran, Russia, Georgia, Armenia, Turkey (a tiny stretch via Nakhchivan); sits between Europe and Asia at the eastern end of the Caucasus Mountains. (3)

Outline: roughly eagle-shaped, with the Absheron Peninsula as the 'beak'. (1)

State oil company: SOCAR — the world's 68th largest company. (1)

Physical Geography

Caspian Sea — the world's largest inland body of water; Baku sits on its western shore; Absheron Peninsula juts in; the Contract of the Century (1994) was a product-sharing oil agreement signed here with 11 foreign companies. (5)

Caucasus Mountains — form the country's northern frame; the Zangezur Mountains mark the western border with Armenia's Syunik province. (3)

Mount Bazardüzü — country's tallest peak; on the northern border. (2)

Five Finger Mountain — in the Absheron region. (2)

Kura River — longest river; flows past Ardahan and Gori before entering Azerbaijan and emptying into Lake Mingachevir. (2)

Aras River — forms the southern border with Iran (and a short frontier with Turkey) and the borders of Nakhchivan. (3)

Gobustan National Park — protects 6,000 prehistoric rock paintings and many mud volcanoes. (2)

Baku

Icheri Shekher (Old City) — UNESCO-listed walled inner city. (1)

Maiden Tower — old-city tower tied to the legend of a maiden ascending from holy fire to save her people. (2)

Heydar Aliyev Center — Zaha Hadid-designed curved-roof landmark named for the first president. (2)

Flame Towers — modern skyline trio. (1)

Oil heritage: the world's first oil well was drilled off the Baku coast; the Nobel and Rothschild families exploited reserves; the Neft Daşları ('Oil Rocks') was the world's first offshore platform; Black Town industrial zone (1882); a pipeline runs from Baku to Batumi, Georgia. (3)

Nakhchivan & Disputed Regions

Nakhchivan Autonomous Republic — Azerbaijani exclave sandwiched between Armenia, Iran, and Turkey; separated from the rest of Azerbaijan by Armenia's Syunik Province; was the largest exclave in the USSR behind Kaliningrad; allegedly founded by Noah after the Flood; Aras River forms its southern border. (4)

Nakhchivan landmarks: Momine Khatun Mausoleum, Gulustan Mausoleum, Noah's Mausoleum; the city of Julfa once held the world's largest collection of khachkar gravestones, destroyed in a 1990s cultural-genocide campaign; birthplace of Heydar Aliyev. (2)

Zangezur Corridor — a proposed transit route across Armenia's Syunik to connect Nakhchivan to the rest of Azerbaijan. (1)

Nagorno-Karabakh — long-disputed region within Azerbaijan; held by the unrecognized Republic of Artsakh (an enclave example) before Azerbaijan's 2020 victory; main city Stepanakert. (4)

Karki — exclave-of-an-exclave village (Nakhchivani) occupied by Armenia; subject of the Lachin Corridor / Bishkek protocol settlement. (1)

People & Culture

Mountain Jews — concentrated in the Quba District's town of Gyrmyzy Gasaba (Qırmızı Qəsəbə), the only all-Jewish settlement outside Israel or the U.S. (2)

Khinalug — village near the Caucasus mountains; one of the world's longest continuously inhabited places; site of a former Zoroastrian shrine. (2)

Shirvanshahs — historical dynasty centered in the region of Shirvan along the Caspian. (2)

Caviar — Azerbaijan is a top producer (with Kazakhstan) of Caspian Beluga sturgeon caviar. (1)

Iranian Azerbaijan — historical region of NW Iran (cities Urmia and Tabriz, Ardabil Province) sharing identity with the country to its north. (1)

Bahamas 18
Commonwealth of The Bahamas · Caribbean (Lucayan Archipelago) · Capital: Nassau
Caribbean

At a Glance

Capital: Nassau — on New Providence Island; named for William of Orange; served by Lynden Pindling International Airport; hosts the world's largest Junkanoo festival. (5)

Flag: black triangle and two blue stripes separated by a band of yellow. (1)

Position: Caribbean archipelago east of Florida, north of Cuba, and just north of the Turks and Caicos Islands; part of the larger Lucayan Archipelago. (5)

History: Columbus's first New World landing was at San Salvador / Guanahani (1492); Nassau later became capital of the pirate Republic of Pirates; slave revolters from the ship Creole settled in Nassau. (3)

Major Islands

Andros Island — largest island; fifth-largest in the Caribbean; west of New Providence; world's highest density of blue holes; explored by Jacques Cousteau alongside the Tongue of the Ocean trench; 1938 red-tide event devastated its sponging industry, prompting emigration to Coconut Grove, Miami. (2)

New Providence — most populous island; contains Nassau and Paradise Island (home of the Atlantis Paradise Resort). (4)

San Salvador — Columbus's first landfall in the Americas; Lucayan name Guanahani. (2)

Exuma chain — district famous for swimming pigs at Big Major Cay. (3)

Bimini — westernmost island (50 mi east of Miami); name means 'twin island' in Lucayan; setting of Hemingway's Islands in the Stream; site of the controversial Bimini Road ('Road to Atlantis'). (2)

Great Inagua, Mayaguana, and Eleuthera — outer islands; Eleuthera was settled by Puritan 'Eleutheran Adventurers'. (3)

Castaway Cay (Disney) and a separate MSC Cruises private island — cruise-line destinations. (1)

Environment & Culture

Caribbean pine ('pineyard') forests dominate the northern islands. (1)

'Conchy Joe' — local term for white Bahamians of European descent. (1)

Bahia 4
State of Brazil · Center of Afro-Brazilian culture · Capital: Salvador
Brazil (States & Regions)

At a Glance

Capital: Salvador — center of Afro-Brazilian culture; hosts one of the world's largest Carnival celebrations. (3)

Name: state translates to 'Bay' — for the Bay of All Saints (Baía de Todos os Santos). (1)

Flag: white triangle in a blue upper-left corner with red-white-blue stripes, inspired by the slave-driven Revolt of the Tailors. (1)

Salvador

Pelourinho — historic core; Olodum percussion ensemble originated here (famous for Michael Jackson's They Don't Care About Us). (2)

Elevador Lacerda — public elevator linking the upper and lower cities. (1)

Bay of All Saints — protected by the São Marcelo Fort and the Barra Lighthouse. (1)

Porto da Barra Beach — rare west-facing beach in Brazil; center of the Tropicália music movement. (1)

Culture & Identity

Candomblé — syncretic Yoruba-Brazilian religion originated here. (1)

Capoeira — Afro-Brazilian martial art / dance form originated here. (2)

Bahrain 12
Kingdom of Bahrain · Persian Gulf island state · Capital: Manama
Middle East & North Africa

At a Glance

Capital: Manama — on the Persian Gulf; the kingdom is made up of 50 natural and 33 artificial islands. (4)

King Fahd Causeway — links Bahrain (Al Jasar) to Khobar in Saudi Arabia; longest international bridge in the world; Passport Island at the midpoint; in 2011 GCC troops crossed it to suppress Shia Arab Spring protests; the planned King Hamad Causeway would relieve traffic. (5)

Driving switch: construction of a King Fahd-named route triggered a switch from left- to right-side driving in Manama. (1)

Islands & Geography

Al-Muharraq Island — site of Bahrain International Airport and the village of Al Qala (with the Bahrain Fort). (3)

Hawar Islands — uninhabited natural-gas-bearing archipelago awarded to Bahrain; supports only a 'Beach Hotel' and a police station; largest Socotra Cormorant colony outside Yemen. (3)

Umm an Nusan — private island reserve of the ruling family. (1)

Sitra & Tubli Bay — wetland west of Sitra, much damaged by land reclamation. (1)

Jabal ad-Dukhan / 'Mountain of Smoke' — country's highest point. (1)

Awali oilfield — main hydrocarbons field, now rapidly depleting. (1)

Landmarks & History

Tree of Life — solitary mesquite tree in the southern desert; national symbol. (2)

Pearl Roundabout — monument of five hyperbolic pillars topped by a giant pearl; demolished by government forces in 2011 during the Arab Spring crackdown. (2)

Bahrain International Circuit — Formula 1 racetrack with a desert-tent grandstand. (1)

Dilmun burial mounds — ancient tumuli from the Bronze Age Dilmun civilization. (1)

NSA Bahrain (Juffair) — strategic U.S. naval base in the Persian Gulf. (1)

Pearl fishing — historic source of Bahraini wealth; oyster-pearl industry comparable to the Gulf of Mannar. (1)

Bangladesh 25
People's Republic of Bangladesh · South Asia · Capital: Dhaka
South Asia

At a Glance

Capital: Dhaka — divided into upazila subdivisions; nicknamed the 'Rickshaw Capital of the World'; suburb of Savar was site of the 2013 Rana Plaza textile-factory collapse. (5)

Government: Sheikh Hasina of the Awami League has led since 1981; her father (Mujibur Rahman) won the 1971 election that triggered the Liberation War; Operation Searchlight was the genocidal Pakistani campaign that preceded independence. (2)

Identity: the only country with Bengali as its sole official language; Pahela Falgun is its spring festival (women wear yellow); was known as East Pakistan before 1971. (3)

Density: the most densely populated country on mainland Asia. (1)

Dhaka

Buriganga River — flows past the city, heavily polluted by sewage. (2)

Ahsan Manzil — distinct pink palace along the Buriganga; once the nawab's residence. (2)

Lalbagh Fort — incomplete 17th-century Mughal fort. (1)

National Assembly Building — designed by Louis I. Kahn; near the Zia Uddyan mausoleum of Mujibur Rahman; revolution that birthed the city began at a Maidan in its Armenian quarter (Rose Garden Palace). (1)

Narayanganj — port serving Dhaka. (1)

Geography & Rivers

Ganges–Brahmaputra Delta — the country sits in the world's largest delta; the Brahmaputra (called Yarlung Tsangpo in Tibet) joins the Padma (Bengali for Ganges) north of Dhaka; flooding is endemic. (4)

Bay of Bengal — south coast; receives the deltas of the Ganges and Brahmaputra. (2)

Sundarbans — vast mangrove forest of the delta; shared with India's West Bengal; home to Bengal tigers; total ban on hunting; the Bhagabatpur Crocodile Project is headquartered here. (4)

Karnaphuli River — flows through Chittagong; spanned by the Shah Amanat Bridge. (2)

St. Martin's Island — southernmost point; at the mouth of the Naf River near the Myanmar border. (1)

Cities & Regions

Chittagong / Chattogram — second-largest city and principal port; near Myanmar; in the namesake Chittagong Hill Tracts tribal region (bordered to the east by the Kaladan River Valley); houses the world's largest shipbreaking yard (20% of global demand); independence declared from the Kalurghat radio station in 1971. (4)

Cox's Bazar — world's longest natural sea beach; hosts the Kutupalong Rohingya refugee camp — the world's largest. (4)

Sylhet Division — tea/oil-industry heartland; source of most British-Bangladeshi emigrants; Jaflong stone industry fuels a construction boom. (2)

Sixty Dome Mosque — UNESCO site built by the Bengal Sultanate from its old capital Gauda. (1)

Manga District / Rangpur — disputed area along the Radcliffe Line with India. (2)

Hazards & History

1989 Daulatpur–Saturia tornado — the deadliest tornado in human history. (1)

Cyclone Bhola (1970) — devastating cyclone whose mishandled response helped trigger independence; Cyclone Sidr later devastated the Sundarbans. (2)

The Concert for Bangladesh — 1971 George Harrison benefit concert raised relief for the new nation. (1)

Telecom & microfinance: Grameenphone (telecom) is a joint venture with the Grameen Bank microfinance institution. (1)

Barbados 8
Caribbean (Lesser Antilles) · Capital: Bridgetown
Caribbean

At a Glance

Capital: Bridgetown — harbor framed by Needhams Point and Cheapside; Independence Arch spans the Careenage; Constitution River empties into the harbor; Browne's Beach fronts the shipwreck-strewn Carlisle Bay; St. Michael's Cathedral (repeatedly destroyed by hurricanes). (6)

People: locals known as Bajans; notable natives include singer Rihanna (monument in Saint Michael parish). (4)

Flag: blue and yellow with a black trident. (3)

Status: left the Commonwealth realm to become a republic in 2021. (1)

Position & Geography

Easternmost island of the Lesser Antilles in the Caribbean. (3)

Limestone geology — contrasts with the volcanic landscape of neighbors like Saint Vincent; Harrison's Cave is carved into the limestone. (1)

Landmarks & History

George Washington House — the only place George Washington stayed outside the present-day U.S. (he visited as a 19-year-old in 1751). (3)

Mount Gay Rum Distillery (1703) — world's oldest rum brand. (2)

Sandy Lane Resort — luxury resort visited by Jacqueline Kennedy. (1)

Emancipation Statue — honors Bussa, leader of a major 1816 slave revolt. (1)

Colonial heritage: during the 1600s the most prosperous British colony in the Americas; many white settlers later relocated to the Carolinas. (1)

Beijing 7
Capital of the People's Republic of China · Direct-controlled municipality
China (Regions)

At a Glance

Name: Beijing means 'Northern Capital'; also called Peking. (2)

Megalopolis: part of the Jingjinji region incorporating Hebei Province and Tianjin. (1)

Streets: narrow alleys called hutong. (1)

Olympics: first city ever to host both the Summer Olympics (2008) and Winter Olympics (2022); features the Bird's Nest Stadium. (2)

Imperial Beijing

Forbidden City — Ming/Qing imperial palace; on the central axis; built under the Yongle Emperor; centered on the Hall of Supreme Harmony. (4)

Tiananmen Square — central public square; the Gate of Heaven-Sent Peace (Tiananmen) marks its north side. (2)

Beihai Park — overlooked by a white stupa. (2)

Summer Palace — Qing imperial retreat. (1)

Jingshan Park — artificial hill behind the Forbidden City. (1)

Temple of Heaven — ritual altar (model for Chongqing's Great Hall of the People). (1)

Modern Beijing

Bird's Nest National Stadium — 2008/2022 Olympic centerpiece. (2)

Zaha Hadid buildings: Leeza SOHO (600-ft atrium), Galaxy SOHO, Wangjing SOHO. (1)

Daxing International Airport — Zaha Hadid's huge starfish-shaped airport. (1)

Badaling — most-visited section of the Great Wall, near Beijing. (1)

Tianqiao — neighborhood south of the Forbidden City. (1)

Chaobai River drains the area. (1)

Belarus 21
Republic of Belarus · Eastern Europe (landlocked) · Capital: Minsk
Europe

At a Glance

Capital: Minsk — on the Svislach and Niamiha Rivers; home to the Belarusian Ridge. (5)

Government: ruled since 1994 by Alexander Lukashenko; the only European country still practicing the death penalty; 2020 election protests followed the loss of opposition candidate Sviatlana Tsikhanouskaya; Belarus grounded Ryanair flight 4978 (May 2021) to arrest journalist Roman Protasevich; in 2021 helped funnel Iraqi migrants to the Polish border (Kuźnica). (3)

Regions: six oblasts including those centered on Brest, Grodno, Vitebsk, Mogilev, Gomel, and Minsk; pre-Holocaust, Vitebsk/Mogilev/Pinsk each had ~10% Jewish populations. (2)

Flag: independence flag (1991-95) was a red-white-red horizontal triband; replaced by the current red-and-green design with white ornamental pattern. (2)

Minsk Landmarks

Palace of the Republic — constructivist hall colloquially called 'the Sarcophagus'. (1)

Island of Tears — man-made island monument with chapel. (2)

Victory Square — central square with an obelisk capped by the Order of Victory and Sacred Sword. (2)

Niamiha metro station — site of a deadly 1999 stampede during a thunderstorm. (1)

Independence Avenue, Gorky Park, Drazdy district, and the National Library of Belarus. (2)

CIS headquarters — Minsk hosts the administrative HQ of the Commonwealth of Independent States. (2)

Physical Geography

Pripet Marshes / Marshes of Pinsk — Europe's largest wetland; cover much of southern Belarus; called the 'lungs of Europe'; received heavy fallout from Chernobyl. (2)

Białowieża / Belavezha Forest — straddles the Polish border; Europe's last remaining primeval lowland forest; home of the European bison (wisent / zubr); 1991 Belavezha Accords here dissolved the Soviet Union. (3)

Neman River — originates in Belarus and forms a stretch of the Lithuanian border; Belarus's lowest point lies along its upper reaches. (2)

Bug River — flows past Brest (site of the WWI Treaty of Brest-Litovsk). (2)

Daugava (Western Dvina) — drains the northern part of the country en route to the Gulf of Riga. (2)

Dzyarzhynskaya Hara — highest point; originally named 'Holy Mountain' and renamed for Cheka founder Felix Dzerzhinsky. (1)

Cities & Heritage

Brest — western border city; site of the medieval Brest Fortress with a 300-ft obelisk and the carved 'Courage' monument; near Białowieża. (3)

Grodno — northwestern city; afflicted by elevated cancer rates after the Chernobyl plume drifted north. (2)

Pripyat (Ukraine) — across the border; Chernobyl evacuees were relocated and the 1986 radiation plume blanketed southern Belarus. (2)

Cuisine: draniki (potato-and-onion pancakes) are a national staple. (1)

Tech: Israeli programmers Talmon Marco and Igor Magazinik launched Viber here. (1)

Belgium 23
Kingdom of Belgium · Western Europe (Benelux) · Capital: Brussels
Europe

At a Glance

Capital: Brussels — headquarters of the EU, NATO, and the European Council; the Senne River flows through; the Sonian Forest lies nearby. (5)

Flag: vertical black-yellow-red tricolor. (1)

Three regions: Dutch-speaking Flanders in the north, French-speaking Wallonia in the south, and bilingual Brussels; the German-speaking minority lives in the Eupen-Malmedy area; Walloon dialect is spoken in the south. (5)

Border oddities: Belgium exclaves parts of the German town of Monschau after postwar shifts; Wallonia hosts the domestic exclave of Comines-Warneton and five Belgian exclaves of Germany along the defunct Vennbahn railway; Flemish Baarle-Hertog is a famous exclave-in-the-Netherlands. (2)

Brussels

Atomium — 1958 Expo landmark of steel balls modeled on an iron crystal. (2)

Manneken Pis — small bronze fountain statue of a urinating boy. (2)

Grand-Place — central square; home to the first Godiva Chocolates shop. (1)

Magritte Museum — devoted to surrealist René Magritte. (1)

Brussels sprouts — the city's namesake cabbage. (1)

Flanders

Antwerp — largest Flemish city, second-largest in Belgium; on the Scheldt River near its North Sea mouth; world diamond-trading hub; second-busiest European port. (4)

Ghent, Bruges, Ypres — medieval cloth-making cities; Bruges has a Michelangelo Madonna and famous castles; Ypres is the setting of John McCrae's 'In Flanders Fields'. (4)

Leuven — university city in Brabant province; HQ of AB InBev, the world's largest brewer. (3)

North Sea ports: Ostend (namesake of the Austrian Empire's Ostend Company) and Zeebrugge. (2)

Flanders provinces: West Flanders, East Flanders, Antwerp, Limburg, Flemish Brabant; Vlaams Belang is the regionalist nationalist party. (1)

Wallonia

Spa — town in the Ardennes that gave English the word for any thermal resort. (2)

Mons, Namur, Charleroi, Liège — major Walloon cities; Mons and Charleroi are in Hainaut Province. (3)

Symbol: Wallonia is represented by a red rooster. (2)

Geography

Ardennes Forest — densely wooded hills shared with Luxembourg and France; site of the WWII Battle of the Bulge and the Malmedy massacre; setting of Shakespeare's As You Like It; the Botrange is its highest point. (3)

Meuse River — flows past Charleroi and Liège. (2)

Scheldt River — flows through Antwerp to the North Sea. (3)

Yser River — flows through a Flemish 'goose-foot' (Ganzenpoot) drainage system. (1)

Rupel, Dyle, Senne — additional Flemish rivers; the Dyle hosts Mechelen. (1)

Industry

Chocolate — Belgium is one of the world's premier chocolate producers (along with Switzerland). (1)

Diamonds — Antwerp's diamond district is a global center. (2)

Belize 26
Central America / Caribbean coast · Capital: Belmopan
Central America

At a Glance

Capital: Belmopan — name is a portmanteau of Belize and the Mopan River; the capital was moved inland from Belize City after Hurricane Hattie (1961). (3)

History: former colony of British Honduras; the only English-speaking country in Central America; the right to harvest logwood here was guaranteed by the 1783 Treaty of Versailles, with the northern border fixed along the Rio Hondo. (5)

Political parties: the PUP and UDP were both co-founded by activist Philip Goldson, namesake of the country's main north-south highway. (1)

Borders: Mexico (Quintana Roo) to the north, Guatemala (Petén/Izabal) to the west — long-disputed via the Sarstoon River; Caribbean Sea to the east. (4)

Coast, Reef & Caves

Belize Barrier Reef — second-longest reef system in the world; UNESCO World Heritage Site; affected by coral bleaching of A. tenuifolia; in 2010 Belize became the first country to ban bottom trawling in its EEZ. (4)

Great Blue Hole — 407-ft circular marine sinkhole in the middle of Lighthouse Reef; named by Ned Middleton; popularized by Jacques Cousteau; stalactites within prove its dry-cave origin. (4)

Half Moon Caye — partner natural-monument inside the same UNESCO site. (1)

Ambergris Caye — largest island; ring of white-sand beaches around a mangrove swamp; main town San Pedro; named for grey amber; south of Mexico's Xcalak Peninsula; near Corozal Bay. (4)

Caye Caulker — limestone coral island south of Ambergris. (2)

Glover's Reef — atoll within the barrier reef system. (1)

Districts & Cities

Cayo District — western district; contains the second-largest city San Ignacio and Mayan ruins at Caracol, Baking Pot, and Xunantunich; Barton Creek is a Mennonite community originating from the Vistula River region. (3)

Orange Walk District — northern district containing Fort Mundy's remains and the town of Orange Walk; the New River (longest entirely within the country) flows through it into Chetumal Bay. (3)

Belize City — most populous city; Haulover Creek runs along the Philip Goldson Highway; supports about half the ancient Mayan Caracol population. (2)

Mayan Heritage

Caracol — ancient Mayan city in the Cayo District; once supported twice the population of modern Belize City. (2)

Xunantunich, Baking Pot, Lamanai — major archaeological sites scattered through the country. (1)

People

Garifuna — Afro-indigenous people who refer to their ancestral homeland as Yurumein; descended from Arawaks and Africans formerly on St. Vincent, exiled via Baliceaux to Roatán; now also live along the Belizean and Honduran coast; many practice the syncretic Dügü faith. (1)

Mennonites — Plautdietsch-speaking settlers in Cayo (Barton Creek) and elsewhere. (1)

Benin 10
Republic of Benin · West Africa · Capital: Porto-Novo
Sub-Saharan Africa

At a Glance

Capital: Porto-Novo — official capital; the larger Cotonou is the seat of government and largest city. (5)

Former name: the Republic of Dahomey (Kingdom of Dahomey was the powerful Fon precolonial state); current name borrowed from the Bight of Benin on the Gulf of Guinea (the namesake Benin Kingdom itself sits in modern Nigeria). (4)

Borders: Togo, Burkina Faso, Niger (via the Niger River), and Nigeria. (2)

Cities & Regions

Abomey — historic seat of the kings of Dahomey; UNESCO-listed Royal Palaces of Abomey include the palaces of Glélé and Ghézo, the sword Goubassa, the 'jar of unity', and the cowrie house; in Zou Department. (1)

Ouidah — Atlantique Department city; memorial arch 'Door of No Return' marks the embarkation point of the Atlantic slave trade. (1)

Grand-Popo — southwestern salient and major Vodun (voodoo) center; name from Yoruba word for 'west'. (2)

Ganvie — stilt-village on Lake Nokoué; nicknamed the 'Venice of Africa'; hosts the annual Voodoo Festival. (1)

W National Park — tri-national park (Benin/Niger/Burkina Faso) at the northern extremity, on the Niger River. (1)

People & Culture

Fon — largest ethnic group; speak Fon; primary practitioners of Vodun (Voodoo). (3)

Ogu — people of the eastern portion of the country. (1)

Voodoo — official religion; Benin is the cradle of the Vodun faith from which Caribbean voodoo descends. (2)

Bhutan 16
Kingdom of Bhutan ('Druk Yul' / 'Thunder Dragon') · Eastern Himalayas · Capital: Thimphu
South Asia

At a Glance

Capital: Thimphu — on the Wang Chhu River in the Himalayas; landmarks include Tashichho Dzong and the National Memorial Chorten. (5)

Monarchy: ruled by the Wangchuck Dynasty ('Dragon Kings'); first king Ugyen signed a treaty in Punakha; coined the concept of Gross National Happiness in 1972. (4)

Language & symbols: national language Dzongkha (closely related to Sikkimese); flag features the Thunder Dragon Druk. (3)

Firsts: the only fully organic farming country; banned tobacco in 2010 (first country to do so); banned television until 1999. (2)

Physical Geography

Gangkhar Puensum — the world's tallest unclimbed mountain; lies inside Bhutan. (6)

Himalayas — fill the country's north; Chele La is the highest navigable pass. (2)

Black Mountains — central spine; source of the Mo Chhu and Drangme Chhu river systems. (2)

Dochula Pass — site of the 108 Druk Wangyal Chortens honoring 2003's Operation All Clear against Assamese separatists. (1)

Monasteries & Dzongs

Paro Taktsang / Tiger's Nest — clifftop Vajrayana monastery in the Paro Valley (route by which Buddhism entered Bhutan from Tibet). (4)

Dzongs — white-walled fortress-monasteries that housed the ruling class; shared name with Tibetan counties; the Wangchuk dynasty rose from Trongsa Dzong; Kurje Monastery has winter quarters in this style. (2)

People & History

Ethnic groups: indigenous Sharchops in the east, later-arriving Ngalops in the west; the Lhotshampa ('Southerners') minority has been targeted for deportation since 1980 (over 100,000 expelled). (2)

Dress: traditional gho for men and kira for women. (1)

Unification: Ngawang Namgyal ('Shabdrung') unified Bhutan after conquering the Lhapa kingdom and winning the Battle of Five Lamas; received Portuguese Jesuit missionaries. (1)

Duar War / Treaty of Sinchula (1865) — Bhutan ceded the Cooch Behar Duars to Britain; pre-modern governance used governors called ponlops and a regent called the Druk Desi. (2)

Economy

Hydropower — nearly half of Bhutan's exports are electricity sold to India; the 90-ft Tala Power Station is a major source. (1)

Bolivia 44
Plurinational State of Bolivia · South America (landlocked Andean) · Capitals: Sucre (constitutional) & La Paz (seat of government)
South America

At a Glance

Capitals (two): Sucre is the constitutional capital; La Paz is the seat of government and the world's highest national capital. (6)

Government: named after Simón Bolívar; modern era marked by leader Evo Morales and the early-2000s Cochabamba water-privatization protests (which inspired Quantum of Solace). (2)

Landlocked status: lost its Pacific coast (including Antofagasta) to Chile in the War of the Pacific; one of only two landlocked South American countries (with Paraguay). (3)

Borders: Brazil, Paraguay, Argentina, Chile, and Peru (across Lake Titicaca). (4)

La Paz

Setting: sits in a valley of the Andean Altiplano beneath Mount Illimani in the Cordillera Real; Aymara name Chuqi Yapu; connected to El Alto by cable car to form the world's highest major urban area. (5)

Plaza Murillo — central square with the Palacio Quemado ('Burnt Palace', so called since an 1875 fire) and the Plurinational Legislative Assembly. (3)

Calle Sagárnaga — steep marketplace patrolled by yatiri (Aymara seers) — the famous Witches' Market. (1)

Valle de la Luna — eroded badland 'Valley of the Moon' on the city's outskirts. (1)

Physical Geography

Altiplano — high Andean plateau; named for the Qulla people; source of the Diablada ('Dance of the Devils') with siku panpipes. (2)

Andes / Cordillera Real / Cordillera Oriental — eastern Andean ranges crossing the country. (2)

Lake Titicaca — shared with Peru; world's highest navigable lake; contains the Island of the Sun and Amantaní; floating Uros reed islands; Strait of Tiquina connects its two basins; the Aymara people inhabit its shores; ports include Puno and Guaqui. (5)

Lake Poopo — second-largest lake; dried up in 1994 and again in 2016; receives the Desaguadero River from Titicaca; drains into Lake Coipasa via the Lacajahuira River; near the Wankarani culture region. (1)

Salar de Uyuni — world's largest salt flat; in Potosí Department; largest remnant of ancient Lake Minchin; contains cactus-covered Incahuasi Island; holds ~7% of world lithium reserves; doubled as planet Crait in The Last Jedi. (3)

Pantanal — vast wetland (mostly in Brazil) extending into Bolivia. (2)

Gran Chaco — semi-arid lowland in the southeast, shared with Paraguay and Argentina; Izozog Swamp at its northern end; backed the Chaco War. (1)

Yungas Road — notoriously deadly cliff road; Madidi National Park has over 10% of all bird species; the Chacaltaya ski resort was the world's highest before its glacier melted. (2)

Cities & Mining

Potosí — colonial silver-mining city beneath Cerro Rico (controlled by the demon El Tío in local belief); over half the world's silver was mined here 1550–1600; home to the Casa de Moneda mint (possibly the origin of the dollar sign). (3)

Cochabamba — third-largest city; site of the 2000 water-privatization protests. (1)

San Vicente — Andean town where Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid likely died. (1)

Pre-Columbian Heritage

Tiwanaku — pre-Inca site on Titicaca's shore; contains the Gate of the Sun monolith. (2)

Aymara and Quechua — two largest indigenous nations; both languages share official status with Spanish. (3)

Bosnia and Herzegovina 14
Bosna i Hercegovina · Balkans · Capital: Sarajevo
Balkans

At a Glance

Capital: Sarajevo — on the Miljacka River in the Dinaric Alps; hosted the 1984 Winter Olympics; subject of the Dayton Agreement (1995). (5)

Structure: the southernmost European country (besides Switzerland) divided into cantons. (1)

Coastline: the Neum Strip is the country's only access to the Adriatic Sea, splitting Croatian Dalmatia in two. (3)

Borders: almost entirely enclosed by Croatia (north and southwest), with Serbia and Montenegro to the east. (3)

Sarajevo Landmarks

Latin Bridge — site of the 1914 assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand by Gavrilo Princip of the Black Hand. (4)

Gazi Husrev-beg Mosque & Ferhat Pasha Mosque — major Ottoman mosques. (2)

Avaz Twist Tower — tallest building in the Balkans. (2)

Goat's Bridge & Yellow Fortress — Ottoman-era city landmarks. (1)

Trebević Mountain — Olympic mountain accessed by cable car; hosted bobsled in 1984. (2)

Operation Irma — wartime medical evacuation during the Siege of Sarajevo. (1)

Physical Geography

Dinaric Alps — backbone of the country's terrain. (2)

Sava River — forms the northern border with Croatia; receives the Bosna, Drina, Una, and Vrbas rivers. (2)

Drina River — eastern border with Serbia. (1)

Neretva River — flows through Herzegovina to the Adriatic; spanned by Stari Most (Old Bridge) in Mostar (destroyed in the 1990s war, rebuilt as the regional symbol). (1)

Regions & Cities

Herzegovina — southern historical region centered on Mostar; includes the Trebinje District and the Neum Strip. (1)

Mostar — largest city of Herzegovina; on the Neretva River. (1)

Tuzla — third-largest city in the country. (1)

Neum — only Bosnian coastal town. (2)

Botswana 24
Republic of Botswana · Southern Africa (landlocked) · Capital: Gaborone
Sub-Saharan Africa

At a Glance

Capital: Gaborone — replaced Mafikeng as capital in 1965 at independence from the Bechuanaland Protectorate; overlooked by Kgale Hill ('the Sleeping Giant'); on the Notwane River; HQ of the Southern African Development Community. (5)

Currency: the Pula ('rain') — replaced the South African rand in the 1970s. (2)

Borders: South Africa to the south (via the Limpopo River), Namibia, Zambia (at the Caprivi/Kazungula crossing), and Zimbabwe. (3)

The Kalahari & Drylands

Kalahari Desert — covers most of the country; name means 'the great thirst' (in Tswana / 'dry place' in Khoekhoe); contains the Makgadikgadi Salt Pan (once Lake Makgadikgadi, considered the origin of Homo sapiens); ancient dry riverbeds called omuramba; home to Dragon's Breath Cave (world's largest underground lake), Drotsky's / Gcwihaba Cave (source of gwihabaite mineral), and Tsodilo Hills (over 4,500 rock paintings). (8)

Kalahari Game Reserves: the binational Kgalagadi Transfrontier Park (with South Africa); the Central Kalahari Game Reserve; Khutse and Tswalu reserves protect gemsbok. (2)

Okavango Delta & Waterways

Okavango Delta — vast inland delta in the endorheic Kalahari basin; UNESCO-listed; the river splits into Thaoge, Xudum, and Thamakalane distributaries; Chiefs Island is its largest arable expanse; Moremi Game Reserve lies to the east; over 300 elephants died in 2019 from cyanobacteria poisoning. (4)

Okavango River — called the Cubango in Angola; flows past Rundu in Namibia before turning south. (3)

Lake Ngami — fed by the Taoghe distributary; remnant of ancient Lake Makgadikgadi. (2)

Limpopo River — southern border; high silt content gave it Kipling's 'great grey-green, greasy' nickname; receives the Shashe tributary. (3)

Cities & Mining
